The country's eastern regions, especially around <a href="/wiki/Debrecen" title="Debrecen">Debrecen</a> (the "Calvinist Rome"), remain almost completely <a href="/wiki/Reformed" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed">Reformed</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup> a trait they share with historically contiguous <a href="/wiki/Hungarians_in_Romania" title="Hungarians in Romania">ethnically Hungarian regions</a> across the Romanian border. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Christianity</a> in Hungary is associated with the country's ethnic minorities: <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgarians" title="Bulgarians">Bulgarians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanians" title="Romanians">Romanians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rusyns" title="Rusyns">Rusyns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians" title="Ukrainians">Ukrainians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a>. </p>
      <p>Historically, Hungary was home to a <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Hungary" title="History of the Jews in Hungary">significant Jewish community</a> with a pre-<a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> population of more than 800,000, but it is estimated that just over 564,000 Hungarian Jews were killed between 1941 and 1945 during <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust_in_Hungary" title="The Holocaust in Hungary">the Holocaust in Hungary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup> Between 15 May and 9 July 1944 alone, over 434,000 Jews were deported on 147 trains,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBraham2016771,_774–775_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBraham2016771,_774–775-207">[206]</a></sup> most of them to <a href="/wiki/Auschwitz" class="mw-redirect" title="Auschwitz">Auschwitz</a>, where about 80 percent were gassed on arrival. Some Jews were able to escape, but most were either deported to concentration camps, where they were killed or murdered in Hungary by <a href="/wiki/Arrow_Cross_Party" title="Arrow Cross Party">Arrow Cross</a> members. From over 800,000 Jews living within Hungary's borders in 1941–1944, about 255,500 are thought to have survived. There are about 120,000 Jews in Hungary today.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Hungary" title="Education in Hungary">Education in Hungary</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Education in Hungary is predominantly public, run by the <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Education_(Hungary)" title="Minister of Education (Hungary)">Ministry of Education</a>. <a href="/wiki/Preschool" title="Preschool">Preschool</a>-<a href="/wiki/Kindergarten" title="Kindergarten">kindergarten</a> education is <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_education" title="Compulsory education">compulsory</a> and provided for all children between three and six years old, after which school attendance is also <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_education" title="Compulsory education">compulsory</a> until the age of sixteen.<sup id="cite_ref-europa_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europa-29">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Primary_education" title="Primary education">Primary education</a> usually lasts for eight years. <a href="/wiki/Secondary_education" title="Secondary education">Secondary education</a> includes three traditional types of schools focused on different academic levels: the <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">Gymnasium</a> enrolls the most gifted children and prepares students for university studies; the secondary vocational schools for intermediate students lasts four years and the technical school prepares pupils for <a href="/wiki/Vocational_training" class="mw-redirect" title="Vocational training">vocational education</a> and the world of work. The system is partly flexible and bridges exist, graduates from a vocational school can achieve a two years programme to have access to vocational higher education for instance.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Trends_in_International_Mathematics_and_Science_Study" title="Trends in International Mathematics and Science Study">Trends in International Mathematics and Science Study</a> (TIMSS) rated 13–14-year-old pupils in Hungary among the bests in the world for maths and science. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Iskola-Lotz3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Iskola-Lotz3.JPG/220px-Iskola-Lotz3.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Iskola-Lotz3.JPG/330px-Iskola-Lotz3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Iskola-Lotz3.JPG/440px-Iskola-Lotz3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2432"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Iskola-Lotz3.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Rector's Council Hall of <a href="/wiki/Budapest_Business_School" title="Budapest Business School">Budapest Business School</a>, the first public <a href="/wiki/Business_school" title="Business school">business school</a> in the world, founded in 1857
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Most of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_Hungary" class="mw-redirect" title="List of universities in Hungary">Hungarian universities</a> are <a href="/wiki/Public_university" title="Public university">public institutions</a>, and students traditionally study without fee payment. The general requirement for university is the <a href="/wiki/Matura" title="Matura">Matura</a>. The Hungarian public <a href="/wiki/Higher_education" title="Higher education">higher education</a> system includes universities and other higher education institutes, that provide both education curricula and related degrees up to doctoral degree and also contribute to research activities. Health insurance for students is free until the end of their studies. English and German language are important in Hungarian higher education, there are a number of degree programmes that are taught in these languages, which attracts thousands of <a href="/wiki/Student_exchange_program" title="Student exchange program">exchange students</a> every year. Hungary's higher education and training has been ranked 44 out of 148 countries in the Global Competitiveness Report 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Hungary has a long tradition of higher education reflecting the existence of established <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_economy" title="Knowledge economy">knowledge economy</a>. The established <a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_Hungary" class="mw-redirect" title="List of universities in Hungary">universities in Hungary</a> include some of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_oldest_universities_in_continuous_operation" title="List of oldest universities in continuous operation">oldest in the world</a>, the first was the <a href="/wiki/University_of_P%C3%A9cs" title="University of Pécs">University of Pécs</a> founded in 1367 which is still functioning, although, in the year 1276, the university of <a href="/wiki/Veszpr%C3%A9m" title="Veszprém">Veszprém</a> was destroyed by the troops of <a href="/wiki/Peter_I_Cs%C3%A1k" title="Peter I Csák">Peter Csák</a>, but it was never rebuilt. <a href="/wiki/Sigismund" title="Sigismund">Sigismund</a> established <a href="/wiki/%C3%93buda_University" title="Óbuda University">Óbuda University</a> in 1395. Another, <a href="/wiki/Universitas_Istropolitana" title="Universitas Istropolitana">Universitas Istropolitana</a>, was established 1465 in <a href="/wiki/Pozsony" class="mw-redirect" title="Pozsony">Pozsony</a> by <a href="/wiki/Mattias_Corvinus" class="mw-redirect" title="Mattias Corvinus">Mattias Corvinus</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nagyszombat" class="mw-redirect" title="Nagyszombat">Nagyszombat</a> University was founded in 1635 and moved to Buda in 1777 and it is called <a href="/wiki/E%C3%B6tv%C3%B6s_Lor%C3%A1nd_University" title="Eötvös Loránd University">Eötvös Loránd University</a> today. The world's first <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_technology" title="Institute of technology">institute of technology</a> was founded in <a href="/wiki/Selmecb%C3%A1nya" class="mw-redirect" title="Selmecbánya">Selmecbánya</a>, the Kingdom of Hungary in 1735, its legal successor is the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Miskolc" title="University of Miskolc">University of Miskolc</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Budapest_University_of_Technology_and_Economics" title="Budapest University of Technology and Economics">Budapest University of Technology and Economics</a> is considered the oldest institute of technology in the world with university rank and structure, its legal predecessor the Institutum Geometrico-Hydrotechnicum was founded in 1782 by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Joseph II, Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor Joseph II</a>. </p>
      <p>Hungary ranks fourth (above neighbour Romania, and after China, the United States and Russia) in the all-time medal count at the <a href="/wiki/International_Mathematical_Olympiad" title="International Mathematical Olympiad">International Mathematical Olympiad</a> with 336 total medals, dating back to 1959. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Hungary" title="Healthcare in Hungary">Healthcare in Hungary</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Szentistvankorhazcivertanlegi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Szentistvankorhazcivertanlegi.jpg/250px-Szentistvankorhazcivertanlegi.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Szentistvankorhazcivertanlegi.jpg/375px-Szentistvankorhazcivertanlegi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/Szentistvankorhazcivertanlegi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="325"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Szentistvankorhazcivertanlegi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Szent István Kórház (<a href="/wiki/Stephen_I_of_Hungary" title="Stephen I of Hungary">Saint Stephen</a> Hospital) at <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Cll%C5%91i_Avenue" class="mw-redirect" title="Üllői Avenue">Üllői Avenue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a>. With Szent László Kórház (<a href="/wiki/Ladislaus_I_of_Hungary" title="Ladislaus I of Hungary">Saint Ladislaus</a> Hospital) making the largest hospital complex in Hungary, built at the turn of the 19th and 20th century.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Hungary maintains a <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a> system largely financed by government <a href="/wiki/National_health_insurance" title="National health insurance">national health insurance</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a>, 100% of the population is <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_health_insurance_coverage" title="List of countries by health insurance coverage">covered by universal health insurance</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-auto_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-28">[27]</a></sup> which is absolutely free for <a href="/wiki/Children" class="mw-redirect" title="Children">children</a>, <a href="/wiki/Student" title="Student">students</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pensioner" title="Pensioner">pensioners</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">people with low income</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disability" title="Disability">handicapped people</a>, and church employees.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[211]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[212]</a></sup> Hungary spends 7.2% of GDP on healthcare, spending $2,045 per capita, of which $1,365 is provided by the government.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[213]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Hungary is one of the main destinations of medical tourism in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Dental_tourism" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental tourism">dental tourism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBJ1_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBJ1-216">[215]</a></sup> in which its share is 42% in Europe and 21% worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-BBJ1_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBJ1-216">[215]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBJ2_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBJ2-217">[216]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plastic_surgery" title="Plastic surgery">Plastic surgery</a> is also a key sector, with 30% of the clients coming from abroad. Hungary is well known for its spa culture and is home to <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Hungary#Spa_culture" title="Culture of Hungary">numerous medicinal spas</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-IMTJ_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMTJ-218">[217]</a></sup> which attract "spa tourism".<sup id="cite_ref-BBJ3_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBJ3-219">[218]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In common with developed countries, <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a> is a leading cause of mortality, accounting for 49.4% (62,979) of all deaths in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-KSH_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KSH-220">[219]</a></sup> However, this number peaked in 1985 with 79,355 deaths, and has been declining continuously since the fall of Communism.<sup id="cite_ref-KSH_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KSH-220">[219]</a></sup> The second leading cause of death is <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> with 33,274 (26.2%), which has been stagnant since the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-KSH_220-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KSH-220">[219]</a></sup> Deaths from <a href="/wiki/Accident" title="Accident">accidents</a> dropped from 8,760 in 1990 to 3,654 in 2013; the number of <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicides</a> has declined precipitously from 4,911 in 1983 to 2,093 in 2013 (21.1 per 100,000 people), the lowest since 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-KSH_220-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KSH-220">[219]</a></sup> There are considerable health disparities between the western and eastern parts of Hungary; <a href="/wiki/Heart_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a> is prevalent in the mostly <a href="/wiki/Agricultural" class="mw-redirect" title="Agricultural">agricultural</a> and low-income <a href="/wiki/Great_Hungarian_Plain" title="Great Hungarian Plain">Great Plain</a> region in the east, but infrequent in the high-income, <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a> areas of <a href="/wiki/Western_Transdanubia" title="Western Transdanubia">Western Transdanubia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Hungary" title="Central Hungary">Central Hungary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[220]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Smoking" title="Smoking">Smoking</a> is a leading cause of death in the country, although it is in steep decline: The proportion of adult smokers declined to 19% in 2013 from 28% in 2012, owing to strict regulations such as a nationwide smoking ban in every indoor public place and the limiting of tobacco sales to state-controlled "National Tobacco Shops".<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[221]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Hungary ranks as the <a href="/wiki/Global_Peace_Index" title="Global Peace Index">17th safest country</a> in the world, with a homicide rate of 1.3 per 100,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[222]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Hungary" title="Culture of Hungary">Culture of Hungary</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Hungary" title="Architecture of Hungary">Architecture of Hungary</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Esterh%C3%A1zy-kast%C3%A9ly_(4051._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k)_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Esterh%C3%A1zy-kast%C3%A9ly_%284051._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k%29_2.jpg/310px-Esterh%C3%A1zy-kast%C3%A9ly_%284051._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k%29_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="310" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Esterh%C3%A1zy-kast%C3%A9ly_%284051._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k%29_2.jpg/465px-Esterh%C3%A1zy-kast%C3%A9ly_%284051._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k%29_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Esterh%C3%A1zy-kast%C3%A9ly_%284051._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k%29_2.jpg/620px-Esterh%C3%A1zy-kast%C3%A9ly_%284051._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k%29_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="10303" data-file-height="4077"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Esterh%C3%A1zy-kast%C3%A9ly_(4051._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k)_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Eszterh%C3%A1za" title="Eszterháza">Eszterháza Palace</a>, the "Hungarian Versailles" in <a href="/wiki/Fert%C5%91d" title="Fertőd">Fertőd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gy%C5%91r-Moson-Sopron_County" title="Győr-Moson-Sopron County">Győr-Moson-Sopron County</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Hungary is home to the largest synagogue in Europe (<a href="/wiki/Doh%C3%A1ny_Street_Synagogue" title="Dohány Street Synagogue">Great Synagogue</a>), built in 1859 in Moorish Revival style with a capacity of 3,000 people, the largest medicinal bath in Europe (<a href="/wiki/Sz%C3%A9chenyi_Medicinal_Bath" class="mw-redirect" title="Széchenyi Medicinal Bath">Széchenyi Medicinal Bath</a>), completed in 1913 in Modern Renaissance Style and located in the Budapest city park, the biggest building in Hungary with its 268 metres (879 feet) length (the Parliament building), one of the largest basilicas in Europe (<a href="/wiki/Esztergom_Basilica" title="Esztergom Basilica">Esztergom Basilica</a>), the second-largest territorial abbey in the world (<a href="/wiki/Pannonhalma_Archabbey" title="Pannonhalma Archabbey">Pannonhalma Archabbey</a>), and the largest early Christian necropolis outside Italy (<a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9cs" title="Pécs">Pécs</a>). </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ap%C3%A1ts%C3%A1gi_templom_(8941._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k)_7.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Ap%C3%A1ts%C3%A1gi_templom_%288941._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k%29_7.jpg/220px-Ap%C3%A1ts%C3%A1gi_templom_%288941._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k%29_7.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Ap%C3%A1ts%C3%A1gi_templom_%288941._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k%29_7.jpg/330px-Ap%C3%A1ts%C3%A1gi_templom_%288941._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k%29_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Ap%C3%A1ts%C3%A1gi_templom_%288941._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k%29_7.jpg/440px-Ap%C3%A1ts%C3%A1gi_templom_%288941._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k%29_7.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1556" data-file-height="1032"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ap%C3%A1ts%C3%A1gi_templom_(8941._sz%C3%A1m%C3%BA_m%C5%B1eml%C3%A9k)_7.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Romanesque <a href="/w/index.php?title=J%C3%A1k_Abbey&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ják Abbey (page does not exist)">Ják Abbey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vas_County" title="Vas County">Vas County</a>, built between 1220 and 1256.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Notable architectural styles in Hungary include <a href="/wiki/Historicism" title="Historicism">Historicism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau" title="Art Nouveau">Art Nouveau</a>, or rather several variants of Art Nouveau. In contrast to Historicism, Hungarian Art Nouveau is based on national architectural characteristics. Taking the eastern origins of the Hungarians into account, <a href="/wiki/%C3%96d%C3%B6n_Lechner" title="Ödön Lechner">Ödön Lechner</a> (1845–1914), the most important figure in Hungarian Art Nouveau was initially inspired by Indian and Syrian architecture, and later by traditional Hungarian decorative designs. In this way, he created an original synthesis of architectural styles. By applying them to three-dimensional architectural elements, he produced a version of Art Nouveau that was specific to Hungary. </p>
      <p>Turning away from the style of Lechner, yet taking inspiration from his approach, the group of "Young People" (<i>Fiatalok</i>), which included <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1roly_K%C3%B3s" title="Károly Kós">Károly Kós</a> and Dezsö Zrumeczky, were to use the characteristic structures and forms of traditional Hungarian architecture to achieve the same end. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Museum_of_Applied_Arts_(Budapest).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Museum_of_Applied_Arts_%28Budapest%29.jpg/220px-Museum_of_Applied_Arts_%28Budapest%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Museum_of_Applied_Arts_%28Budapest%29.jpg/330px-Museum_of_Applied_Arts_%28Budapest%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Museum_of_Applied_Arts_%28Budapest%29.jpg/440px-Museum_of_Applied_Arts_%28Budapest%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="778"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Museum_of_Applied_Arts_(Budapest).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Applied_Arts_(Budapest)" title="Museum of Applied Arts (Budapest)">Museum of Applied Arts</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau" title="Art Nouveau">Art Nouveau</a> building designed by <a href="/wiki/%C3%96d%C3%B6n_Lechner" title="Ödön Lechner">Ödön Lechner</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Besides the two principal styles, Budapest also displays local versions of trends originating from other European countries. The Sezession from <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, the German <a href="/wiki/Jugendstil" title="Jugendstil">Jugendstil</a>, Art Nouveau from Belgium and France, and the influence of English and Finnish architecture are all reflected in the buildings constructed at the turn of the 20th century. <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_Lajta" title="Béla Lajta">Béla Lajta</a> initially adopted Lechner's style, subsequently drawing his inspiration from English and Finnish trends; after developing an interest in the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> style, he finally arrived at modern architecture. <a href="/wiki/Alad%C3%A1r_%C3%81rkay" title="Aladár Árkay">Aladár Árkay</a> took almost the same route. <a href="/wiki/Istv%C3%A1n_Medgyaszay" title="István Medgyaszay">István Medgyaszay</a> developed his own style, which differed from Lechner's, using stylised traditional motifs to create decorative designs in concrete. In the sphere of applied arts, those chiefly responsible for promoting the spread of Art Nouveau were the School and Museum of Decorative Arts, which opened in 1896. </p>
      <p>Foreigners have unexpectedly "discovered" that a significantly large portion of the citizens lives in old and architecturally valuable buildings. In the <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a> downtown area almost all the buildings are about one hundred years old, with thick walls, high ceilings, and motifs on the front wall.<sup id="cite_ref-Eliznik.org.uk_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eliznik.org.uk-44">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[223]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Hungary" title="Music of Hungary">Music of Hungary</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Hungarian_State_Opera_House(PDXdj).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Hungarian_State_Opera_House%28PDXdj%29.jpg/220px-Hungarian_State_Opera_House%28PDXdj%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Hungarian_State_Opera_House%28PDXdj%29.jpg/330px-Hungarian_State_Opera_House%28PDXdj%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Hungarian_State_Opera_House%28PDXdj%29.jpg/440px-Hungarian_State_Opera_House%28PDXdj%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="681"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Hungarian_State_Opera_House(PDXdj).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Hungarian_State_Opera_House" title="Hungarian State Opera House">Hungarian State Opera House</a> on <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A1ssy_%C3%BAt" title="Andrássy út">Andrássy út</a> (UNESCO World Heritage Site)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Hungarian music consists mainly of traditional <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_folk_music" title="Hungarian folk music">Hungarian folk music</a> and music by prominent composers such as <a href="/wiki/Liszt" class="mw-redirect" title="Liszt">Liszt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bart%C3%B3k" class="mw-redirect" title="Bartók">Bartók</a>, considered to be among the greatest Hungarian composers. Other renowned composers are <a href="/wiki/Ern%C5%91_Dohn%C3%A1nyi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ernő Dohnányi">Dohnányi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Schmidt" title="Franz Schmidt">Franz Schmidt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zolt%C3%A1n_Kod%C3%A1ly" title="Zoltán Kodály">Zoltán Kodály</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_von_Wayditch" title="Gabriel von Wayditch">Gabriel von Wayditch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Wagner-R%C3%A9geny" title="Rudolf Wagner-Régeny">Rudolf Wagner-Régeny</a>, <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A1szl%C3%B3_Lajtha" title="László Lajtha">László Lajtha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Leh%C3%A1r" title="Franz Lehár">Franz Lehár</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imre_K%C3%A1lm%C3%A1n" class="mw-redirect" title="Imre Kálmán">Imre Kálmán</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1ndor_Veress" title="Sándor Veress">Sándor Veress</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mikl%C3%B3s_R%C3%B3zsa" title="Miklós Rózsa">Rózsa</a>. Hungarian traditional music tends to have a strong <a href="/wiki/Dactyl_(poetry)" title="Dactyl (poetry)">dactylic</a> rhythm, as the language is invariably stressed on the first syllable of each word. </p>
      <p>Hungary has renowned composers of contemporary classical music, <a href="/wiki/Gy%C3%B6rgy_Ligeti" title="György Ligeti">György Ligeti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gy%C3%B6rgy_Kurt%C3%A1g" title="György Kurtág">György Kurtág</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_E%C3%B6tv%C3%B6s" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter Eötvös">Péter Eötvös</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zolt%C3%A1n_Kod%C3%A1ly" title="Zoltán Kodály">Zoltán Kodály</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zolt%C3%A1n_Jeney" title="Zoltán Jeney">Zoltán Jeney</a> among them. One of the greatest Hungarian composers, <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_Bart%C3%B3k" title="Béla Bartók">Béla Bartók</a>, was also among the most significant musicians of the 20th century. His music was invigorated by the themes, modes, and rhythmic patterns of the Hungarian and neighbouring folk music traditions he studied, which he synthesised with influences from his contemporaries into his own distinctive style.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[224]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Liszt_1858.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Liszt_1858.jpg/170px-Liszt_1858.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="344" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Liszt_1858.jpg/255px-Liszt_1858.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Liszt_1858.jpg/340px-Liszt_1858.jpg 2x" data-file-width="667" data-file-height="1351"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Liszt_1858.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Ferenc (Franz) Liszt</a>, one of the greatest pianists of all time; well-known composer and conductor
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Hungary has made many contributions to the fields of <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_classical_music" class="mw-redirect" title="European classical music">classical music</a>. Hungarian folk music is a prominent part of the national identity and continues to play a major part in Hungarian music. Hungarian folk music has been significant in former country parts that belong&nbsp;– since the 1920 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Trianon" title="Treaty of Trianon">Treaty of Trianon</a>&nbsp;– to neighbouring countries such as Romania, Slovakia, Poland and especially in southern Slovakia and <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">Transylvania</a>; both regions have significant numbers of Hungarians. After the establishment of a music academy led by <a href="/wiki/Ferenc_Erkel" title="Ferenc Erkel">Ferenc Erkel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Franz Liszt</a> Hungary produced an important number of art musicians: </p> 
      <ul>
       <li>Pianists: <a href="/wiki/Ern%C3%B6_von_Dohn%C3%A1nyi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ernö von Dohnányi">Ernő von Dohnányi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ervin_Nyiregyh%C3%A1zi" title="Ervin Nyiregyházi">Ervin Nyiregyházi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andor_F%C3%B6ldes" title="Andor Földes">Andor Földes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tam%C3%A1s_V%C3%A1s%C3%A1ry" title="Tamás Vásáry">Tamás Vásáry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gy%C3%B6rgy_S%C3%A1ndor" title="György Sándor">György Sándor</a>, <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9za_Anda" title="Géza Anda">Géza Anda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Annie_Fischer" title="Annie Fischer">Annie Fischer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gy%C3%B6rgy_Cziffra" title="György Cziffra">György Cziffra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Kilenyi" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward Kilenyi">Edward Kilényi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balint_Vazsonyi" title="Balint Vazsonyi">Bálint Vázsonyi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A1s_Schiff" title="András Schiff">András Schiff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zolt%C3%A1n_Kocsis" title="Zoltán Kocsis">Zoltán Kocsis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dezs%C5%91_R%C3%A1nki" title="Dezső Ránki">Dezső Ránki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jen%C5%91_Jand%C3%B3" title="Jenő Jandó">Jenő Jandó</a> and others.</li> 
       <li>Violists: <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Joachim" title="Joseph Joachim">Joseph Joachim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Auer" title="Leopold Auer">Leopold Auer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jen%C5%91_Hubay" title="Jenő Hubay">Jenő Hubay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jelly_d%27Ar%C3%A1nyi" title="Jelly d'Arányi">Jelly d'Arányi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Szigeti" title="Joseph Szigeti">Joseph Szigeti</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1ndor_V%C3%A9gh" title="Sándor Végh">Sándor Végh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emil_Telmanyi" class="mw-redirect" title="Emil Telmanyi">Emil Telmanyi</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zathurecky&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Zathurecky (page does not exist)">Ede Zathurecky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zsigmondy" title="Zsigmondy">Zsigmondy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franz_von_Vecsey" title="Franz von Vecsey">Franz von Vecsey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zolt%C3%A1n_Sz%C3%A9kely" title="Zoltán Székely">Zoltán Székely</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tibor_Varga_(violinist)" title="Tibor Varga (violinist)">Tibor Varga</a> and newcomers <a href="/wiki/Antal_Szalai" class="mw-redirect" title="Antal Szalai">Antal Szalai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vilmos_Szabadi" title="Vilmos Szabadi">Vilmos Szabadi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krist%C3%B3f_Bar%C3%A1ti" title="Kristóf Baráti">Kristóf Baráti</a> (b. 79) and others.</li> 
       <li>Opera singers: <a href="/wiki/Astrid_Varnay" title="Astrid Varnay">Astrid Varnay</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zsef_Sim%C3%A1ndy" title="József Simándy">József Simándy</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BAlia_V%C3%A1rady" title="Júlia Várady">Júlia Várady</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julia_Hamari" title="Julia Hamari">Júlia Hamari</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kolos_Kov%C3%A1ts&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kolos Kováts (page does not exist)">Kolos Kováts</a> (Bluebeard in Bartók's Bluebeard)</li> 
       <li>Conductors: <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Ormandy" title="Eugene Ormandy">Eugene Ormandy</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Szell" title="George Szell">George Szell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antal_Dor%C3%A1ti" title="Antal Doráti">Antal Doráti</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_Ferencsik" title="János Ferencsik">János Ferencsik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Reiner" title="Fritz Reiner">Fritz Reiner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Georg_Solti" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Georg Solti">sir Georg Solti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istv%C3%A1n_Kert%C3%A9sz_(conductor)" title="István Kertész (conductor)">István Kertész</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferenc_Fricsay" title="Ferenc Fricsay">Ferenc Fricsay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zolt%C3%A1n_Rozsnyai" title="Zoltán Rozsnyai">Zoltán Rozsnyai</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1ndor_V%C3%A9gh" title="Sándor Végh">Sándor Végh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arpad_Joo" class="mw-redirect" title="Arpad Joo">Árpád Joó</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%81d%C3%A1m_Fischer" title="Ádám Fischer">Ádám Fischer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iv%C3%A1n_Fischer" title="Iván Fischer">Iván Fischer</a>, <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9ter_E%C3%B6tv%C3%B6s" title="Péter Eötvös">Péter Eötvös</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zolt%C3%A1n_Kocsis" title="Zoltán Kocsis">Zoltán Kocsis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tam%C3%A1s_V%C3%A1s%C3%A1ry" title="Tamás Vásáry">Tamás Vásáry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Varga" title="Gilbert Varga">Gilbert Varga</a> and others</li> 
       <li>String Quartets: <a href="/wiki/Budapest_Quartet" class="mw-redirect" title="Budapest Quartet">Budapest Quartet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Quartet" title="Hungarian Quartet">Hungarian Quartet</a>, <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A9gh_Quartet" title="Végh Quartet">Végh Quartet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tak%C3%A1cs_Quartet" title="Takács Quartet">Takács Quartet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kod%C3%A1ly_Quartet" title="Kodály Quartet">Kodály Quartet</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%89der_Quartet&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Éder Quartet (page does not exist)">Éder Quartet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Festetics_Quartet" class="mw-redirect" title="Festetics Quartet">Festetics Quartet</a>,</li>
      </ul> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bart%C3%B3k_B%C3%A9la_1927.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Bart%C3%B3k_B%C3%A9la_1927.jpg/170px-Bart%C3%B3k_B%C3%A9la_1927.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Bart%C3%B3k_B%C3%A9la_1927.jpg/255px-Bart%C3%B3k_B%C3%A9la_1927.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Bart%C3%B3k_B%C3%A9la_1927.jpg/340px-Bart%C3%B3k_B%C3%A9la_1927.jpg 2x" data-file-width="411" data-file-height="379"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bart%C3%B3k_B%C3%A9la_1927.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_Bart%C3%B3k" title="Béla Bartók">Béla Bartók</a>, an influential composer from the early 20th century; one of the founders of <a href="/wiki/Ethnomusicology" title="Ethnomusicology">ethnomusicology</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Broughton claims that Hungary's "infectious sound has been surprisingly influential on neighboring countries (thanks perhaps to the common Austro-Hungarian history) and it's not uncommon to hear Hungarian-sounding tunes in Romania, Slovakia and Poland".<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[225]</a></sup> It is also strong in the <a href="/wiki/Szabolcs-Szatm%C3%A1r-Bereg" class="mw-redirect" title="Szabolcs-Szatmár-Bereg">Szabolcs-Szatmár</a> area and in the southwest part of <a href="/wiki/Transdanubia" title="Transdanubia">Transdanubia</a>, near the border with Croatia. The <a href="/wiki/Bus%C3%B3j%C3%A1r%C3%A1s" title="Busójárás">Busójárás</a> carnival in <a href="/wiki/Moh%C3%A1cs" title="Mohács">Mohács</a> is a major Hungarian folk music event, formerly featuring the long-established and well-regarded <a href="/wiki/Bogyiszl%C3%B3_orchestra" class="mw-redirect" title="Bogyiszló orchestra">Bogyiszló orchestra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Broughton_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broughton-227">[226]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Hungarian <a href="/wiki/European_classical_music" class="mw-redirect" title="European classical music">classical music</a> has long been an "experiment, made from Hungarian antecedents and on Hungarian soil, to create a conscious musical culture [using the] musical world of the folk song".<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[227]</a></sup> Although the Hungarian upper class has long had cultural and political connections with the rest of Europe, leading to an influx of European musical ideas, the rural peasants maintained their own traditions such that by the end of the 19th-century Hungarian composers could draw on rural peasant music to (re)create a Hungarian classical style.<sup id="cite_ref-Szabolcsi_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Szabolcsi-229">[228]</a></sup> For example, Bartók collected folk songs from across Central and Eastern Europe, including Romania and Slovakia, while Kodály was more interested in creating a distinctively Hungarian musical style. </p>
      <p>During the era of communist rule in Hungary (1944–1989), a Song Committee scoured and censored popular music for traces of subversion and ideological impurity. Since then, however, the Hungarian music industry has begun to recover, producing successful performers in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> such as trumpeter <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Tomsits" title="Rudolf Tomsits">Rudolf Tomsits</a>, pianist-composer <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1roly_Binder" title="Károly Binder">Károly Binder</a> and, in a modernised form of Hungarian folk, <a href="/wiki/Ferenc_Seb%C5%91" title="Ferenc Sebő">Ferenc Sebő</a> and <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1rta_Sebesty%C3%A9n" title="Márta Sebestyén">Márta Sebestyén</a>. The three giants of Hungarian rock, <a href="/wiki/Ill%C3%A9s_(band)" title="Illés (band)">Illés</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metr%C3%B3_(band)" class="mw-redirect" title="Metró (band)">Metró</a> and <a href="/wiki/Omega_(band)" title="Omega (band)">Omega</a>, remain very popular, especially Omega, which has followings in Germany and beyond as well as in Hungary. Older veteran underground bands such as <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_(band)" title="Beatrice (band)">Beatrice</a>, from the 1980s, also remain popular. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_literature" title="Hungarian literature">Hungarian literature</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Szekely_Hungarian_Rovas_alphabet_Szekely_magyar_rovas_ABC.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Szekely_Hungarian_Rovas_alphabet_Szekely_magyar_rovas_ABC.svg/390px-Szekely_Hungarian_Rovas_alphabet_Szekely_magyar_rovas_ABC.svg.png" decoding="async" width="390" height="99" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Szekely_Hungarian_Rovas_alphabet_Szekely_magyar_rovas_ABC.svg/585px-Szekely_Hungarian_Rovas_alphabet_Szekely_magyar_rovas_ABC.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Szekely_Hungarian_Rovas_alphabet_Szekely_magyar_rovas_ABC.svg/780px-Szekely_Hungarian_Rovas_alphabet_Szekely_magyar_rovas_ABC.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1028" data-file-height="260"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Szekely_Hungarian_Rovas_alphabet_Szekely_magyar_rovas_ABC.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The alphabet of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Hungarian_script" title="Old Hungarian script">Székely-Hungarian Rovás script</a>; the country switched to using the Latin alphabet under king <a href="/wiki/Stephen_I_of_Hungary" title="Stephen I of Hungary">Saint Stephen</a> (reign: 1000–1038)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the earliest times, <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian language</a> was written in a <a href="/wiki/Old_Hungarian_script" title="Old Hungarian script">runic-like script</a> (although it was not used for literature purposes in the modern interpretation). The country switched to the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> after being Christianized under the reign of <a href="/wiki/Stephen_I_of_Hungary" title="Stephen I of Hungary">Stephen I of Hungary</a> (1000–1038).<br> The oldest remained written record in Hungarian language is a fragment in the <a href="/wiki/Establishing_charter_of_the_abbey_of_Tihany" title="Establishing charter of the abbey of Tihany">Establishing charter of the abbey of Tihany</a> (1055) which contains several Hungarian terms, among them the words <i>feheruuaru rea meneh hodu utu rea</i>, "up the military road to <a href="/wiki/Sz%C3%A9kesfeh%C3%A9rv%C3%A1r" title="Székesfehérvár">Fehérvár</a>" The rest of the document was written in Latin.<br> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ohlm_original.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Ohlm_original.jpg/170px-Ohlm_original.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Ohlm_original.jpg/255px-Ohlm_original.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Ohlm_original.jpg/340px-Ohlm_original.jpg 2x" data-file-width="697" data-file-height="908"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ohlm_original.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The oldest extant Hungarian poem, <a href="/wiki/Old_Hungarian_%27Lamentations_of_Mary%27" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Hungarian 'Lamentations of Mary'">Old Hungarian Laments of Mary</a> (1190s)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The oldest remaining complete text in Hungarian language is the <a href="/wiki/Funeral_Sermon_and_Prayer" title="Funeral Sermon and Prayer">Funeral Sermon and Prayer</a> <i>(Halotti beszéd és könyörgés)</i> (1192–1195), a translation of a Latin sermon.<br> The oldest remaining poem in Hungarian is the <a href="/wiki/Old_Hungarian_%27Lamentations_of_Mary%27" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Hungarian 'Lamentations of Mary'">Old Hungarian Laments of Mary</a> <i>(Ómagyar Mária-siralom)</i>, also a (not very strict) translation from Latin, from the 13th century. It is also the oldest surviving <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a> poem.<br> Among the first chronicles about Hungarian history were <a href="/wiki/Gesta_Hungarorum" title="Gesta Hungarorum">Gesta Hungarorum</a> ("Deeds of the Hungarians") by the unknown author usually called <i>Anonymus</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Gesta_Hunnorum_et_Hungarorum" title="Gesta Hunnorum et Hungarorum">Gesta Hunnorum et Hungarorum</a> ("Deeds of the Huns and the Hungarians") by Simon Kézai. Both are in Latin. These chronicles mix history with legends, so historically they are not always authentic. Another chronicle is the <i>Képes krónika</i> (Illustrated Chronicle), which was written for <a href="/wiki/Louis_I_of_Hungary" title="Louis I of Hungary">Louis the Great</a>. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> literature flourished under the reign of <a href="/wiki/Matthias_Corvinus_of_Hungary" class="mw-redirect" title="Matthias Corvinus of Hungary">King Matthias</a> (1458–1490). <a href="/wiki/Janus_Pannonius" title="Janus Pannonius">Janus Pannonius</a>, although he wrote in Latin, counts as one of the most important persons in Hungarian literature, being the only significant Hungarian Humanist poet of the period. The first printing house was also founded during Matthias' reign, by <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A1s_Hess" title="András Hess">András Hess</a>, in Buda. The first book printed in Hungary was the <a href="/wiki/Chronica_Hungarorum" title="Chronica Hungarorum">Chronica Hungarorum</a>. The most important poets of the period was <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1lint_Balassi" title="Bálint Balassi">Bálint Balassi</a> (1554–1594) and <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Zrinski" class="mw-redirect" title="Nikola Zrinski">Miklós Zrínyi</a> (1620–1664). </p>
      <p>Balassi's poetry shows medieval influences, his poems can be divided into three sections: love poems, war poems and religious poems. Zrínyi's most significant work, the epic <i><a href="/wiki/Peril_of_Sziget" class="mw-redirect" title="Peril of Sziget">Szigeti veszedelem</a></i> ("Peril of Sziget", written in 1648/49) is written in a fashion similar to the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i>, and recounts the heroic <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Szigetv%C3%A1r" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Szigetvár">Battle of Szigetvár</a>, where his great-grandfather died while defending the castle of Szigetvár. Among the religious literary works the most important is the Bible translation by <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A1sp%C3%A1r_K%C3%A1roli" title="Gáspár Károli">Gáspár Károli</a> (The second Hungarian Bible translation in the history), the Protestant pastor of <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6nc" title="Gönc">Gönc</a>, in 1590. The translation is called the <i>Bible of Vizsoly</i>, after the town where it was first published. (See <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Hungarian" title="Bible translations into Hungarian">Bible translations into Hungarian</a> for more details.) </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Petofi.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Petofi.gif/200px-Petofi.gif" decoding="async" width="200" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Petofi.gif/300px-Petofi.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Petofi.gif/400px-Petofi.gif 2x" data-file-width="675" data-file-height="888"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Petofi.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1ndor_Pet%C5%91fi" title="Sándor Petőfi">Sándor Petőfi</a>, a Hungarian poet and revolutionary.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Marai2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Marai2.jpg/170px-Marai2.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Marai2.jpg/255px-Marai2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Marai2.jpg/340px-Marai2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="444" data-file-height="627"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Marai2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1ndor_M%C3%A1rai" title="Sándor Márai">Sándor Márai</a>, Hungarian writer and journalist
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Hungarian enlightenment took place about fifty years after the French <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>. The first enlightened writers were <a href="/wiki/Archduchess_Maria_Theresa_of_Austria_(1717%E2%80%931780)" class="mw-redirect" title="Archduchess Maria Theresa of Austria (1717–1780)">Maria Theresia</a>'s bodyguards (<a href="/wiki/Gy%C3%B6rgy_Bessenyei" title="György Bessenyei">György Bessenyei</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_Bats%C3%A1nyi" title="János Batsányi">János Batsányi</a> and others). The greatest poets of the time were <a href="/wiki/Mih%C3%A1ly_Csokonai_Vit%C3%A9z" class="mw-redirect" title="Mihály Csokonai Vitéz">Mihály Csokonai Vitéz</a> and <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1niel_Berzsenyi" title="Dániel Berzsenyi">Dániel Berzsenyi</a>. The greatest figure of the language reform was <a href="/wiki/Ferenc_Kazinczy" title="Ferenc Kazinczy">Ferenc Kazinczy</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian language</a> became feasible for all type of scientific explanations from this time, and furthermore, many new words were coined for describing new inventions. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Hungarian_literature" title="Hungarian literature">Hungarian literature</a> has recently gained some renown outside the borders of Hungary (mostly through translations into German, French and English). Some modern Hungarian authors have become increasingly popular in Germany and Italy especially <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1ndor_M%C3%A1rai" title="Sándor Márai">Sándor Márai</a>, <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9ter_Esterh%C3%A1zy" title="Péter Esterházy">Péter Esterházy</a>, <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9ter_N%C3%A1das" title="Péter Nádas">Péter Nádas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imre_Kert%C3%A9sz" title="Imre Kertész">Imre Kertész</a>. The latter is a contemporary Jewish writer who survived the Holocaust and won the Nobel Prize for literature in 2002. The older classics of Hungarian literature and Hungarian poetry have remained almost totally unknown outside Hungary. <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_Arany" title="János Arany">János Arany</a>, a famous 19th-century Hungarian poet, is still much loved in Hungary (especially his collection of <a href="/wiki/Ballads" class="mw-redirect" title="Ballads">Ballads</a>), among several other "true classics" like <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1ndor_Pet%C5%91fi" title="Sándor Petőfi">Sándor Petőfi</a>, the poet of the Revolution of 1848, <a href="/wiki/Endre_Ady" title="Endre Ady">Endre Ady</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mih%C3%A1ly_Babits" title="Mihály Babits">Mihály Babits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dezs%C5%91_Kosztol%C3%A1nyi" title="Dezső Kosztolányi">Dezső Kosztolányi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Attila_J%C3%B3zsef" title="Attila József">Attila József</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mikl%C3%B3s_Radn%C3%B3ti" title="Miklós Radnóti">Miklós Radnóti</a> and <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_Pilinszky" title="János Pilinszky">János Pilinszky</a>. Other well-known Hungarian authors are <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A1szl%C3%B3_Krasznahorkai" title="László Krasznahorkai">László Krasznahorkai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferenc_M%C3%B3ra" title="Ferenc Móra">Ferenc Móra</a>, <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9za_G%C3%A1rdonyi" title="Géza Gárdonyi">Géza Gárdonyi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zsigmond_M%C3%B3ricz" title="Zsigmond Móricz">Zsigmond Móricz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gyula_Illy%C3%A9s" title="Gyula Illyés">Gyula Illyés</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Wass" title="Albert Wass">Albert Wass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mikl%C3%B3s_Szentkuthy" title="Miklós Szentkuthy">Miklós Szentkuthy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magda_Szab%C3%B3" title="Magda Szabó">Magda Szabó</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Vizinczey" title="Stephen Vizinczey">Stephen Vizinczey</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_cuisine" title="Hungarian cuisine">Hungarian cuisine</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_wine" title="Hungarian wine">Hungarian wine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beer_in_Hungary" title="Beer in Hungary">Beer in Hungary</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Hortobagy_palacsinta.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Hortobagy_palacsinta.JPG/220px-Hortobagy_palacsinta.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Hortobagy_palacsinta.JPG/330px-Hortobagy_palacsinta.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Hortobagy_palacsinta.JPG/440px-Hortobagy_palacsinta.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Hortobagy_palacsinta.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Hortob%C3%A1gyi_palacsinta" title="Hortobágyi palacsinta">Hortobágyi palacsinta</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sopron" title="Sopron">Sopron</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Dobos_cake_(Gerbeaud_Confectionery_Budapest_Hungary).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Dobos_cake_%28Gerbeaud_Confectionery_Budapest_Hungary%29.jpg/220px-Dobos_cake_%28Gerbeaud_Confectionery_Budapest_Hungary%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Dobos_cake_%28Gerbeaud_Confectionery_Budapest_Hungary%29.jpg/330px-Dobos_cake_%28Gerbeaud_Confectionery_Budapest_Hungary%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Dobos_cake_%28Gerbeaud_Confectionery_Budapest_Hungary%29.jpg/440px-Dobos_cake_%28Gerbeaud_Confectionery_Budapest_Hungary%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="853"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Dobos_cake_(Gerbeaud_Confectionery_Budapest_Hungary).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Dobos_cake" class="mw-redirect" title="Dobos cake">Dobos cake</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Traditional dishes such as the world-famous <a href="/wiki/Goulash" title="Goulash">Goulash</a> (<i>gulyás</i> stew or <i>gulyás</i> soup) feature prominently in Hungarian cuisine. Dishes are often flavoured with <a href="/wiki/Paprika" title="Paprika">paprika</a> (ground red peppers), a Hungarian innovation.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[229]</a></sup> The paprika powder, obtained from a special type of pepper, is one of the most common spices used in typical Hungarian cuisine. Thick, heavy Hungarian sour cream called <i><a href="/wiki/Tejf%C3%B6l" class="mw-redirect" title="Tejföl">tejföl</a></i> is often used to soften the dishes' flavour. The famous Hungarian hot river fish soup called Fisherman's soup or <i><a href="/wiki/Hal%C3%A1szl%C3%A9" class="mw-redirect" title="Halászlé">halászlé</a></i> is usually a rich mixture of several kinds of poached fish.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[230]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Other dishes are chicken paprikash, <a href="/wiki/Foie_gras" title="Foie gras">foie gras</a> made of goose liver, <i><a href="/wiki/P%C3%B6rk%C3%B6lt" title="Pörkölt">pörkölt</a></i> stew, <i>vadas</i>, (game stew with vegetable gravy and <a href="/wiki/Sp%C3%A4tzle" title="Spätzle">dumplings</a>), <a href="/wiki/Trout" title="Trout">trout</a> with almonds and salty and sweet dumplings, like <i><a href="/wiki/T%C3%BAr%C3%B3s_csusza" title="Túrós csusza">túrós csusza</a></i>, (dumplings with fresh <a href="/wiki/Quark_(cheese)" class="mw-redirect" title="Quark (cheese)">quark</a> cheese and thick sour cream). Desserts include the iconic <a href="/wiki/Dobos_Cake" class="mw-redirect" title="Dobos Cake">Dobos Cake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strudel" title="Strudel">strudels</a> (<i>rétes</i>), filled with apple, cherry, poppy seed or cheese, <a href="/wiki/Palacsinta" class="mw-redirect" title="Palacsinta">Gundel pancake</a>, plum dumplings (<i><a href="/wiki/Kl%C3%B6%C3%9Fe" class="mw-redirect" title="Klöße">szilvás gombóc</a></i>), <i>somlói</i> dumplings, dessert soups like chilled <a href="/wiki/Sour_cherry_soup" title="Sour cherry soup">sour cherry soup</a> and sweet chestnut puree, <i>gesztenyepüré</i> (cooked <a href="/wiki/Chestnuts" class="mw-redirect" title="Chestnuts">chestnuts</a> mashed with sugar and rum and split into crumbs, topped with whipped cream). <i><a href="/wiki/Pretzel" title="Pretzel">Perec</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Kifli" title="Kifli">kifli</a></i> are widely popular pastries.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[231]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <i>csárda</i> is the most distinctive type of Hungarian inn, an old-style tavern offering traditional cuisine and beverages. <i>Borozó</i> usually denotes a cosy old-fashioned wine tavern, <i>pince</i> is a beer or wine cellar and a <i>söröző</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Pub" title="Pub">pub</a> offering draught beer and sometimes meals. The <i>bisztró</i> is an inexpensive restaurant often with self-service. The <i>büfé</i> is the cheapest place, although one may have to eat standing at a counter. Pastries, cakes and coffee are served at the confectionery called <i>cukrászda</i>, while an <i>eszpresszó</i> is a café. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Tokaji_6p_1989.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Tokaji_6p_1989.jpg/170px-Tokaji_6p_1989.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Tokaji_6p_1989.jpg/255px-Tokaji_6p_1989.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Tokaji_6p_1989.jpg/340px-Tokaji_6p_1989.jpg 2x" data-file-width="880" data-file-height="1320"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Tokaji_6p_1989.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The famous <a href="/wiki/Tokaji" title="Tokaji">Tokaji</a> wine. It was called "Vinum Regum, Rex Vinorum" ("Wine of Kings, King of Wines") by <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV of France</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/P%C3%A1linka" title="Pálinka">Pálinka</a> is a fruit brandy, distilled from fruit grown in the orchards situated on the <a href="/wiki/Great_Hungarian_Plain" title="Great Hungarian Plain">Great Hungarian Plain</a>. It is a spirit native to Hungary and comes in a variety of flavours including apricot (<i>barack</i>) and cherry (<i>cseresznye</i>). However, plum (<i>szilva</i>) is the most popular flavour. Beer goes well with many traditional Hungarian dishes. The five main Hungarian beer brands are: <a href="/wiki/Borsod_Brewery" title="Borsod Brewery">Borsodi</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Soproni&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Soproni (page does not exist)">Soproni</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Arany_%C3%81szok&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Arany Ászok (page does not exist)">Arany Ászok</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=K%C3%B5b%C3%A1nyai&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kõbányai (page does not exist)">Kõbányai</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dreher_Brewery" class="mw-redirect" title="Dreher Brewery">Dreher</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[232]</a></sup> In Hungary, people traditionally do not clink their glasses or mugs when drinking beer. There is an urban legend in Hungarian culture that Austrian generals clinked their beer glasses to celebrate the execution of <a href="/wiki/The_13_Martyrs_of_Arad" title="The 13 Martyrs of Arad">the 13 Martyrs of Arad</a> in 1849. Many people still follow the tradition, although younger people often disavow it, citing that the vow was only meant to last 150 years.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[233]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Wine: As <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Johnson_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugh Johnson (wine)">Hugh Johnson</a> says in <i>The History of Wine</i>, the territory of Hungary is ideal for wine-making and the country can be divided to six wine regions: <a href="/w/index.php?title=North-Transdanubia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="North-Transdanubia (page does not exist)">North-Transdanubia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Balaton" title="Lake Balaton">Lake Balaton</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=South-Pann%C3%B3nia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="South-Pannónia (page does not exist)">South-Pannónia</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Duna-region&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Duna-region (page does not exist)">Duna-region</a> or Alföld, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Upper-Hungary&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Upper-Hungary (page does not exist)">Upper-Hungary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tokaj-Hegyalja" class="mw-redirect" title="Tokaj-Hegyalja">Tokaj-Hegyalja</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[234]</a></sup> The Romans brought vines to <a href="/wiki/Pannonia" title="Pannonia">Pannonia</a>, and by the 5th century AD, there are records of extensive vineyards in what is now Hungary. The <a href="/wiki/Hungarians" title="Hungarians">Hungarians</a> brought their wine-making knowledge from the East. According to <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Rustah" title="Ahmad ibn Rustah">Ibn Rustah</a>, the Hungarian tribes were familiar with wine-making long time before the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_conquest_of_the_Carpathian_Basin" title="Hungarian conquest of the Carpathian Basin">Hungarian conquest of the Carpathian Basin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[235]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Hungarian wine regions offer a great variety of styles: the main products of the country are elegant and full-bodied dry whites with good acidity, although complex sweet whites (Tokaj), elegant (<a href="/wiki/Eger" title="Eger">Eger</a>) and full-bodied robust reds (<a href="/wiki/Vill%C3%A1ny" title="Villány">Villány</a> and <a href="/wiki/Szeksz%C3%A1rd" title="Szekszárd">Szekszárd</a>). The main varieties are: <a href="/wiki/Olaszrizling" class="mw-redirect" title="Olaszrizling">Olaszrizling</a>, <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A1rslevel%C5%B1" title="Hárslevelű">Hárslevelű</a>, <a href="/wiki/Furmint" title="Furmint">Furmint</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pinot_gris" title="Pinot gris">Pinot gris</a> or Szürkebarát, <a href="/wiki/Chardonnay" title="Chardonnay">Chardonnay</a> (whites), <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A9kfrankos" class="mw-redirect" title="Kékfrankos">Kékfrankos</a> (or Blaufrankisch in German), <a href="/wiki/Kadarka" title="Kadarka">Kadarka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugieser" class="mw-redirect" title="Portugieser">Portugieser</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zweigelt" title="Zweigelt">Zweigelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cabernet_sauvignon" class="mw-redirect" title="Cabernet sauvignon">Cabernet sauvignon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cabernet_franc" class="mw-redirect" title="Cabernet franc">Cabernet franc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merlot" title="Merlot">Merlot</a>. The most famous wines from Hungary are <a href="/wiki/Tokaji_Asz%C3%BA" class="mw-redirect" title="Tokaji Aszú">Tokaji Aszú</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egri_Bikav%C3%A9r" title="Egri Bikavér">Egri Bikavér</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[236]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[237]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tokaji" title="Tokaji">Tokaji</a>, meaning "of Tokaj", or "from Tokaj" in Hungarian, is used to label wines from the wine region of <a href="/wiki/Tokaj-Hegyalja" class="mw-redirect" title="Tokaj-Hegyalja">Tokaj-Hegyalja</a>. Tokaji wine has received accolades from numerous great writers and composers including <a href="/wiki/Beethoven" class="mw-redirect" title="Beethoven">Beethoven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liszt" class="mw-redirect" title="Liszt">Liszt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schubert" class="mw-redirect" title="Schubert">Schubert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Goethe" class="mw-redirect" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>; <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Joseph Haydn</a>'s favourite wine was a Tokaji.<sup id="cite_ref-royal_tokaji_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royal_tokaji-239">[238]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Louis_XV" title="Louis XV">Louis XV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick the Great</a> tried to outdo one another when they entertained guests with Tokaji. Napoleon III, the last Emperor of France, ordered 30–40 barrels of Tokaji at the French Royal Court every year. Gustav III, King of Sweden, loved Tokaji.<sup id="cite_ref-royal_tokaji_239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royal_tokaji-239">[238]</a></sup> In Russia, customers included Peter the Great and Empress Elizabeth, while Catherine the Great actually established a Russian garrison in the town of Tokaj with the aim of assuring regular wine deliveries to St. Petersburg.<sup id="cite_ref-royal_tokaji_239-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royal_tokaji-239">[238]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>For over 150 years, a blend of forty Hungarian herbs has been used to create the liqueur <a href="/wiki/Unicum" title="Unicum">Unicum</a>. Unicum is a bitter, dark-coloured liqueur that can be drunk as an apéritif or after a meal, thus helping the digestion.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[239]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreation">Recreation</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:H%C3%A9v%C3%ADz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/H%C3%A9v%C3%ADz.jpg/220px-H%C3%A9v%C3%ADz.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/H%C3%A9v%C3%ADz.jpg/330px-H%C3%A9v%C3%ADz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/H%C3%A9v%C3%ADz.jpg/440px-H%C3%A9v%C3%ADz.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:H%C3%A9v%C3%ADz.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Lake_H%C3%A9v%C3%ADz" title="Lake Hévíz">Lake Hévíz</a>, the largest thermal lake in Europe
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Hungary is a land of <a href="/wiki/Thermal_bath" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermal bath">thermal</a> water. A passion for spa culture and <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_history" class="mw-redirect" title="Hungarian history">Hungarian history</a> have been connected from the very beginning. Hungarian spas feature <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek architecture">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Turkey" title="Architecture of Turkey">Turkish</a>, and northern country architectural elements.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[240]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Because of an advantageous geographical location, good quality thermal water can be found in great quantities on over 80% of Hungary's territory. Approximately 1,500 thermal springs can be found in Hungary (more than 100 just in the Capital area). There are approximately 450 public baths in Hungary.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[241]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> heralded the first age of spas in Hungary. The remains of their bath complexes are still to be seen in <a href="/wiki/%C3%93buda" title="Óbuda">Óbuda</a>. Spa culture was revived during the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Invasion" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkish Invasion">Turkish Invasion</a> and the thermal springs of <a href="/wiki/Buda" title="Buda">Buda</a> were used for the construction of a number of bathhouses, some of which such as (<a href="/wiki/Kir%C3%A1ly_Baths" title="Király Baths">Király Baths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rudas_Baths" title="Rudas Baths">Rudas Baths</a>) are still functioning. </p>
      <p>In the 19th century, the advancement in deep drilling and medical science provided the springboard for a further leap in bathing culture. Grand spas such as <a href="/wiki/Gell%C3%A9rt_Baths" title="Gellért Baths">Gellért Baths</a>, Lukács Baths, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Island" title="Margaret Island">Margaret Island</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sz%C3%A9chenyi_Medicinal_Bath" class="mw-redirect" title="Széchenyi Medicinal Bath">Széchenyi Medicinal Bath</a> are a reflection of this resurgence in popularity. The Széchenyi Thermal Bath is the largest spa complex in Europe and it was the first thermal bath built in the Pest side of Budapest.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[242]</a></sup> This building is a noted example of modern Renaissance style. Located on the Buda side of Budapest, the Gellért spa is the most famous and luxurious thermal complex of the capital city.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[243]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_art">Folk art</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Voivodina_Hungarians_national_costume_and_dance_6.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Voivodina_Hungarians_national_costume_and_dance_6.jpg/220px-Voivodina_Hungarians_national_costume_and_dance_6.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Voivodina_Hungarians_national_costume_and_dance_6.jpg/330px-Voivodina_Hungarians_national_costume_and_dance_6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Voivodina_Hungarians_national_costume_and_dance_6.jpg/440px-Voivodina_Hungarians_national_costume_and_dance_6.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1302" data-file-height="1340"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Voivodina_Hungarians_national_costume_and_dance_6.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Hungarians dancing <a href="/wiki/Cs%C3%A1rd%C3%A1s" title="Csárdás">csárdás</a> in traditional garments / folk costumes
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Ugr%C3%B3s" title="Ugrós">Ugrós</a> (Jumping dances) are old-style dances dating back to the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. Solo or couple dances accompanied by old-style music, shepherd and other solo man's dances from <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">Transylvania</a>, and marching dances along with remnants of medieval weapon dances belong in this group. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Karik%C3%A1z%C3%B3" title="Karikázó">Karikázó</a> is a circle dance performed by women only accompanied by the singing of folk songs. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Cs%C3%A1rd%C3%A1s" title="Csárdás">Csárdás</a> are new style dances developed in the 18–19th centuries. Csárdás is the Hungarian name for the national dances, with Hungarian embroidered costumes and energetic music. From the men's intricate boot slapping dances to the ancient women's circle dances, Csárdás demonstrates the infectious exuberance of the Hungarian folk dancing still celebrated in the villages. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Verbunkos" title="Verbunkos">Verbunkos</a> is a solo man's dance evolved from the recruiting performances of the <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austro-Hungarian</a> army. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Leg%C3%A9nyes" title="Legényes">Legényes</a> is a men's solo dance done by the ethnic Hungarian people living in the <a href="/wiki/Kalotaszeg" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalotaszeg">Kalotaszeg</a> region of Transylvania. Although usually danced by young men, it can be also danced by older men. The dance is generally performed freestyle by one dancer at a time in front of a band. Women participate in the dance by standing in lines to the side and singing or shouting verses while the men dance. Each man performs a number of points (dance phrases), typically four to eight without repetition. Each point consists of four parts, each lasting four counts. The first part is usually the same for everyone (there are only a few variations). </p>
      <p>It was in the beginning of the 18th-century that the present style of Hungarian folk art took shape, incorporating both <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> elements, depending on the area, as well as Persian <a href="/wiki/Sassanide" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanide">Sassanide</a> influences. Flowers and leaves, sometimes a bird or a spiral ornament, are the principal decorative themes. The most frequent ornament is a flower with a centrepiece resembling the eye of a peacock's feather. </p>
      <p>Nearly all the manifestations of folk art practiced elsewhere in Europe also flourished among the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Hungarian people">Magyar</a> peasantry at one time or another, their ceramics and textile being the most highly developed of all. </p>
      <p>The finest achievements in their textile arts are the embroideries which vary from region to region. Those of <a href="/wiki/Kalotaszeg" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalotaszeg">Kalotaszeg</a> in Transylvania are charming products of Oriental design, sewn chiefly in a single colour&nbsp;– red, blue, or black. Soft in line, the embroideries are applied on altar cloths, pillowcases, and sheets. </p>
      <p>In Hungary proper, Sárköz in <a href="/wiki/Transdanubia" title="Transdanubia">Transdanubia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mez%C5%91k%C3%B6vesd" title="Mezőkövesd">Matyóföld</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Hungarian_Plain" title="Great Hungarian Plain">Great Hungarian Plain</a> produce the finest embroideries. In the <a href="/wiki/Tolna_(county)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tolna (county)">Sárköz</a> region the women's caps show black and white designs as delicate as lace and give evidence of the people's wonderfully subtle artistic feeling. The embroidery motifs applied to women's wear have also been transposed to tablecloths and runners suitable for modern use as wall decorations. </p>
      <p>These vessels, made of black clay, reflect more than three hundred years of traditional Transdanubian folk patterns and shapes. No two are precisely alike, since all work is done by hand, including both the shaping and the decorating. The imprints are made by the thumb or a finger of the ceramist who makes the piece. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Porcelain">Porcelain</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Herend_Porcelain" class="mw-redirect" title="Herend Porcelain">Herend Porcelain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zsolnay" title="Zsolnay">Zsolnay</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Founded in 1826, <a href="/wiki/Herend_Porcelain" class="mw-redirect" title="Herend Porcelain">Herend Porcelain</a> is one of the world's largest ceramic factories, specialising in luxury hand painted and gilded <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a>. In the mid-19th century, it was purveyor to the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburg Dynasty">Habsburg Dynasty</a> and aristocratic customers throughout Europe. Many of its classic patterns are still in production. After the fall of communism in Hungary, the factory was privatised and is now 75% owned by its management and workers, exporting to over 60 countries of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[244]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Zsolnay Porcelain Manufacture is a Hungarian manufacturer of porcelain, pottery, ceramics, tiles and stoneware. The company introduced the eosin glazing process and pyrogranite ceramics. The Zsolnay factory was established by Miklós Zsolnay in <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9cs" title="Pécs">Pécs</a>, Hungary, to produce stoneware and ceramics in 1853. In 1863, his son, <a href="/wiki/Vilmos_Zsolnay" title="Vilmos Zsolnay">Vilmos Zsolnay</a> (1828–1900) joined the company and became its manager and director after several years. He led the factory to worldwide recognition by demonstrating its innovative products at world fairs and international exhibitions, including the 1873 World Fair in Vienna, then at the 1878 World Fair in Paris, where Zsolnay received a Grand Prix. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Hungary_at_the_Olympics" title="Hungary at the Olympics">Hungary at the Olympics</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
